Clause 12 [Statement of guarantee]:

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts moved Amendment No. 10:

    Page 5, line 33, leave out "one year" and insert "three years"

The noble Lord said: As currently worded, the Bill provides that a member of a company limited by guarantee is liable to contribute to the assets of a company only if it is wound up during the time that he is a member or within one year of his ceasing to be a member. This amendment seeks to extend that liability from one year to three years.

The increase in the length of time a person may be liable to contribute will surely increase accountability in such companies and promote long-term thinking by members who have given a guarantee. For example, a member giving a guarantee to a company with a December year-end who resigned immediately after the publication of the year's accounts in, say, February, could be released from his guarantee before any further annual accounts were published. We wonder whether that is sensible or, indeed, fair. The amendment also addresses the concern that companies may take longer than one year to wind up fully. Under the Bill, this would allow members to escape without any liability. I beg to move.

Lord Sainsbury of Turville: This amendment deals with the position of companies limited by guarantee.
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column GC12
 
Such companies are, of course, rather different from companies limited by shares in that in the absence of share capital, provision is made for the members to meet the debts of the company on its winding up to the limit set by the guarantee—for example, £1. Given the absence of share capital, companies of this type are most often, though not exclusively, found in the not-for-profit sector, for example companies serving some kind of community purpose.

Membership of a guarantee company is not transferable and membership cannot be "sold on" as it is with shares. The guarantee disappears with the guarantor, and it is therefore appropriate that members of the company should remain liable for some period after they cease to be members. The current Companies Act places this period at one year. I believe that this remains appropriate.

Of course, in many instances the amount of guarantee is small, if not purely nominal. None the less I would worry a little at the signal we would be sending were we to make a three-fold increase in the period of potential liability. For many guarantee companies—for example, flat management companies, which often take this form—continued involvement in any form long after the member has ceased his or her connection would act as a significant disincentive for people to assume the role of a guarantor. By extending the relevant period to three years, the amendment, if accepted, would impose an unnecessary burden on the guarantor. There seems to be no good reason to increase the present period, which has stood the test of time since the Companies Act 1862.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: I accept the Minister's comments. The advice we have received is that the amounts are normally nominal, as he pointed out. However, a longer period would cause a person to think clearly about the future and the actions he is taking. In those circumstances a three-year period would not be unreasonable. However, this is not an issue on which we wish to score cheap political points. I beg leave to withdraw the amendment.

Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

Lord Sainsbury of Turville: These amendments relate to the important subject of company secretaries. Later provisions in Part 12 set out the law on this subject in detail. Like the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, I believe it would be sensible to wait until we reach that part of the Bill before entering into a substantive debate.

However, the amendments in question touch on an important point about the way in which the Bill handles the issue of company secretaries in private companies. As Members of the Committee know, the Bill proceeds on the basis that the office of company secretary is a requirement for public companies, but not for private companies. It may be that we will debate the policy merits of this position later on, but for the moment I would like to focus on the practical consequences. For a public company, there must always be a company secretary. No company can become a public company without one; and third parties have an interest in knowing who that person is. It is therefore essential that public companies, right from the start, have a secretary and provide the identity of such a person for disclosure on the public register. The Bill provides the mechanism for this.

The question is what should happen where a private company has a secretary. There is no function explicitly reserved to the company secretary in the current legislation. Companies vary widely regarding the responsibilities of their secretaries, with some contracting out what is a main responsibility in others. Under the Bill, whether a private company appoints a person to act in the office of secretary is an entirely voluntary matter and, while many may choose to do so, in formal terms the secretary of a private company will be rather like other officers it may choose to appoint—say, the chairman of the company—
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column GC14
 
occupying what is clearly an office within the company, but one which is not required by law or which the law specifically regulates. Since the appointment of the secretary is a voluntary matter for the company, a private company can change its mind at any time about whether it has or does not have a secretary.

Last spring, we consulted over an approach under which a private company could opt to retain the office of company secretary, with any appointment being notified to Companies House. The response to the consultation was clear: this approach is too complex. As a result of that consultation, we believe that third parties should not be required under any circumstances to know whether a particular private company has a secretary. Therefore, any requirement that a private company secretary be disclosed on the public register would be unnecessary regulation, which we all want to avoid.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: I am disappointed with the Minister. Clause 23 [Entrenched provisions of the articles]:

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts moved Amendment No. 17:

    Page 9, line 8, leave out "all" and insert "90 per cent of"

The noble Lord said: The amendment concerns the entrenched provisions of articles. The Bill introduces a recommendation of the Company Law Review that members of a company should be able to choose to entrench elements of a company's constitution in the
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column GC17
 
articles. However, as currently worded, the provision appears to be a lame duck. The Bill requires unanimous agreement by all members of the company, something which in practice may be extremely difficult to achieve. Under the wording an individual member could block the entrenchment, not even necessarily with any malicious intent. A member of the company may die and until probate has been granted, no one can give assent on behalf of his shares.

The Companies Act 1985 provides that in the case of a takeover there may be a compulsory buyout of any remaining unaccepting shares if the purchasing company receives acceptances from more than 90 per cent of the shares. If a 90 per cent level of acceptances empowers compulsory buyout, a similar percentage should allow for the entrenchment of articles—arguably a less drastic measure. The amendment therefore lowers the threshold at which companies can entrench articles to 90 per cent of members agreeing. That sidesteps the problem of an obstructive and insignificant minority blocking such a motion while still providing security from abuse, in that a majority of 90 per cent must agree with whatever is proposed. I beg to move.

Lord McKenzie of Luton: The ability of companies to entrench certain provisions in their articles is valuable, especially in the light of changes in the Bill to a company's memorandum. The information which, under the Companies Act 1985, is contained in the company's memorandum will, under the Bill, either be in statements made to the registrar on formation, such as the situation of the company's registered office, or in a company's articles, such as the restriction of a company's objects.

Given that some key constitutional information will now be in a company's articles rather than its memorandum, and given that the constitutional material in those articles in effect forms the internal rules of the company, some companies will want to be able to make clear that certain rules are so core to the nature of the company that they cannot be changed at all, or only under restricted conditions. An example would be a special provision restricting the objects of a company. Entrenchment allows this to be done.

Such entrenchment provisions are particularly important in ensuring that potential investors know the nature of the company that they are joining; in other words, that it is a company holding certain provisions and not others as more or less inviolable. Of course, if all the members later decide unanimously that they wish to add further core rules to the set of entrenched provisions and no member can suffer, they should be allowed to do so. The Bill permits this, but it would be a step too far to allow a 90 per cent majority to act in that way. The addition of further entrenched provisions could have the effect of significantly and definitively altering the nature of the company. Allowing the voices of up to 10 per cent of the members to be ignored in those circumstances might be seen as
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column GC18
 
calling into question the basis on which minority members would have signed up to the company and, at least potentially, be open to abuse.

Clause 27 [Registrar to be sent copy of amended articles]:

"(1) Where a company's articles are altered, the company must send to the registrar a copy of the articles as altered not later than 15 days after the alteration takes effect.
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column GC20

The noble Lord said: In moving government Amendment No. 20 I shall speak also to government Amendment No. 40. It is clearly important for the public register to be as accurate and up to date as possible. The Bill helps to ensure that this is the case by triggering an obligation on the company to file information when certain events take place, for example where a company passes a resolution or enters into an agreement affecting its constitution or, in the rather less common case under Clause 36, where a company's constitution will be amended by certain types of enactment.

The difficulty arises because the clauses as currently drafted link the obligation to file a copy of the articles as amended with the obligation to file a copy of the document making or evidencing the amendment or notice of the enactment which makes the change. While this proposition works in the majority of cases, it does not work where, for example, the document making or evidencing the alteration is a conditional resolution and the conditions specified in the resolution have not been met. In those circumstances, the alteration to the articles has not yet taken effect and it is clearly inappropriate and potentially misleading to file a copy of the articles for public disclosure at this stage showing what they would look like once the amendment takes effect in the future.

The amendments to these clauses therefore divorce the requirement to file an up to date copy of the articles from the obligation to file the document which gives rise to the change. Both requirements remain in the Bill, but by ensuring they are separate, we ensure that the obligation to file for example the amended articles bites only once the amendment has taken effect, thus ensuring that the version of the articles which appears on the public register will be the version of the articles that is in force. I beg to move.

4.45 pm

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: Am I right in saying that this redraft actually puts a time scale on filing that is not present in the Bill as originally drafted? Is that the focus of what we are driving at here?

Lord McKenzie of Luton: The focus was that as drafted the measure required both provisions to go together. There could be circumstances where the amendment to the articles has not taken immediate effect because it is dependent on some condition. Therefore, one seeks to separate the requirement for these things to go together.

30 Jan 2006 : Column GC24
 
have a difficulty. If the Minister is not too put off by the partisan nature of the attack on him, I hope that he will accept the amendment.

Lord McKenzie of Luton: I thank the noble Lord, Lord Razzall, for explaining his party's approach to these proceedings. As he and the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, said, there is a long-established principle of current case law that if there is a conflict between a provision contained in a company's memorandum and a provision in its articles, it is the provision in the memorandum which prevails. I apologise for three "provisions" already in the note. That principle sits alongside a number of other common law rules about the relationship between company's memoranda and its articles, all of which comes from a time when a company's memorandum was regarded as its "charter" and its fundamental and generally unalterable law. The position of memoranda under the Bill will be very different; they will be mere historical records of the intention to form a company. In the case of existing companies, their substantive provisions will be deemed by virtue of Clause 29 to be part of their articles.

As the status of the memorandum changes, we have to consider how the old principles established by case law should be applied under the new regime and we need to strike a balance between certainty, convenience and flexibility. We want the status of existing provisions, wherever they are set out, to be made as clear as possible. We do not want the new legislation to oblige companies to reorganise the provisions of their memoranda or articles when they would not otherwise be making changes to them. We do not want to restrict the ability of the courts to reach sensible conclusions in particular cases which we may not have foreseen at this stage.

We do not disagree with the thinking behind the amendment. It addresses a question which is very important, but which is only one of the questions that needs to be addressed in this context. Consider, for example, cases where a provision contained in the memorandum of an existing company immediately before Clause 29 comes into force is contradicted by a provision which is inserted into the articles by a subsequent special resolution. Clause 35 [Effect of company's constitution]:

Lord Sainsbury of Turville moved Amendment No. 38:

    Page 14, line 10, leave out from "if" to end of line 13 and insert "there were covenants, signed and sealed on the part of each member, to observe those provisions."

The noble Lord said: In moving Amendment No. 38, I wish to speak also to Amendment No. 41. These two amendments are both technical and do not make any alteration of substance. Clause 35 replaces Section 14 of the Companies Act 1985, with the only difference being that the reference to a company's "memorandum and articles" in the earlier legislation is replaced with a reference to the company's "constitution" in the new Bill, reflecting the different arrangements under the new Bill. The amendment tidies up the grammar in consequence.

Clause 37 is intended merely to replicate the effect of the provisions of Section 15 of the 1985 Act and this amendment corrects an omission. Section 15(1) prevents a company limited by guarantee, which cannot be incorporated with a share capital, attempting through provisions in its articles or any resolution, to create a share capital by the back door, either by provisions distributing profits in a particular way or by dividing an interest or undertaking into shares. The restriction is on the participation in profits otherwise than as a member. There is no restriction on members of a company limited by guarantee participating in profits. Clause 5 prevents the incorporation of companies limited by guarantee with a share capital, and hence the provisions of Section 15(1) of the Companies Act 1985 are restated in the Bill.

Unfortunately, the crucial words "limited by guarantee" have been inadvertently omitted, with the effect of extending the scope of the provision to all companies without a share capital, and in so doing including some unlimited companies, which are not subject to the equivalent provision of the 1985 Act and which there was no intention of capturing. The amendment corrects this error. I beg to move.

Lord Wedderburn of Charlton moved, as an amendment to Amendment No. 38, Amendment No. 39:

    Line 2, after "part" insert "of the company and"

The noble Lord said: I hope that my noble friend will not find unhelpful my temerity in suggesting an amendment to the Government's formula. Before
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column GC35
 
I give the reasons for moving the amendment, I should say, first, that I apologise for any meeting of the Grand Committee that I am forced to be absent from which is likely to arise in the near future. Secondly, I regret that this important Bill—I almost said, "This hugely important Bill", as it is huge—which is even possibly in some respects a controversial Bill, has been given over to a Grand Committee process. Your Lordships heard recently from the Procedure Committee the reasons why we should try to keep Third Reading for its traditional use. The more Bills that are sent to Grand Committee, the more difficulty your Lordships will have in observing that very good rule.

The reason for this amendment is perhaps of greater importance than at first appears because it tests our attitude to the Bill. Your Lordships will know that the memorandum and articles, which we must now call the constitution of the company, is contractually binding as between each pair of members and as between the company and the member. This is of great importance for various areas of company law, not least the area of personal rights of members to which we shall come later.

The problem was created in previous companies Acts and, indeed, arose as early as 1844 and then in the Act of 1856. Rather than go through the effect of the history I shall quote from the seventh edition of Gower's Principles of Modern Company Law edited by a distinguished company law scholar, Professor Davies, who states at page 58 in regard to the 1856 Act, which installed the memorandum and articles as the constitution:

    "Unhappily, full account was not taken of the vital new factor (namely that the incorporated company was a separate legal entity) and the words 'as if . . . signed and sealed by each member' did not have added to them 'and by the company'. This oddity has survived into the modern Acts".

5.45 pm

When the Bill was printed, it repeated the oddity; namely, that this was a contract between members in inter se. It did not change the traditional formula. However, the traditional formula had been sensibly interpreted by the courts to mean that this contract was binding not only between members but also as between each member and the company. If that had been left as it was, there would be no need to move any amendment to it. But if the Bill ever alters a traditional phrase in company law, the case for interpreting it is not necessarily finally indubitably binding. The Government amendment, which my noble friend moved, does exactly the same with the traditional phrase and states that it is binding as

    "if there were covenants, signed and sealed on the part of each member, to observe those provisions".

I can think of no reason why today we should not state what we mean. We mean what the courts have said it should mean; namely, that it is signed and sealed on the part of the company and of each member to observe those provisions.

That is not mere academic or fanciful exercise. I had thought of situations where it might be raised, but it would leave it, without this amendment, open to a
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column GC36
 
litigant to argue that the new formula means what it says. After all, someone in court is likely to say, "The legislature considered this formula. They not only considered it, signed and sealed by each member, they put it in a new form and retained it. So they must mean what they say. Therefore, the cases from 1915 and onwards that say the company is bound by the contract perhaps are open to question".

We should not waste litigation time and expense with even the remotest possibility of such an argument being acceptable. That is why I beg to move.

Lord Sainsbury of Turville: As the noble Lord, Lord Wedderburn, reminded us, Clause 35 has a long pedigree. It has been part of our company law since 1856—the noble Lord mentioned an earlier date—when it first appeared in Sections 7 and 10 of the Joint Stock Companies Act 1856. It has remained unchanged for 150 years, give or take some changes in the description of the contractual formalities and the new reference to "constitution" which reflects other changes introduced by the Bill.

On the face of it, it may seem odd that the provision refers to the constitution binding both company and its members, but then goes on to describe the contractual relationship which it establishes in terms of covenants signed and sealed by the members only. On the other hand, one might ask whether it would be any less odd to imagine the company signing up to these covenants before the process of registration has brought it into being.

In any event, odd as the approach adopted by the 1856 draftsman and his successors may have been, it was some 60 years before the possible difficulties in his drafting were subjected to comprehensive judicial analysis in the case brought in 1914 by Mr Hickman as a member and flock owner against the Kent and Romney Marsh Sheep Breeders' Association.

The textbooks dwell on the difficulties facing the judge in that case in terms of the working of the legislation and the conflicting authorities citied to him by counsel. But he succeeded in reconciling those authorities and in drawing out three clear principles, which he summarised as follows. First, that no article can constitute a contract between the company and a third person. Secondly, that no right merely purporting to be given by an article to a person in a capacity other than that of a member can be enforced against a company. Thirdly, that articles regulating the rights and obligations of the members generally as such create rights and obligations between them and the company respectively.

Whatever confusion may have existed before the judgment in Hickman's case, the principles that it established have not been seriously challenged in 90 years and have become a well-established part of company law. It is of course true, as the noble Lord, Lord Wedderburn, pointed out almost 50 years ago in his seminal article on the rule in Foss v Harbottle, that some of those principles can lead to anomalous results in particular cases; for example, where a director who is also a member can enforce provisions of the constitution as a member which he would not have
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column GC37
 
been able to enforce merely as a director. But notwithstanding the valuable work of the Company Law Review in this area, we have yet to be convinced that there is anything better and clearer, or indeed as clear, to be put in place of the Hickman rules.

This Bill radically reforms some parts of our company law, but it does not set out to change everything. We do not think that we have an answer to every potential problem in current company law, and we are wary of imposing radical solutions where we are not sure how much of a problem there really is in practice. Moreover, as the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, reminded us, it is important to be clear where we are changing the law and where we are just modernising the drafting. If it is not already clear from the text of Clause 35, I say now that the effect of a company's constitution is not an area in which we want to introduce substantive changes.

Clause 44 [Execution of documents]:

Baroness Turner of Camden: I remind the Committee that if Amendment No. 43 is carried, I will not be able to call Amendments Nos. 44 and 45 by reason of pre-emption.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts moved Amendment No. 43:

    Page 18, line 9, leave out subsection (2).

The noble Lord said: Amendments Nos. 43, 46 and 47 concern the execution of documents and deeds in Clauses 44, 46 and 47. Amendments Nos. 44 and 49 in this group are purely technical. Our eagle-eyed people who gave us assistance on the Bill drew our attention to them, so we thought that we would spread our knowledge a little wider. Perhaps I may leave those as they stand and focus my remarks on Amendments Nos. 43, 46 and 53.
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column GC40
 

This is another issue on which we have had discussions with the Institute of Chartered Secretaries and Administrators. The amendment seeks to simplify the Bill by having the methods by which any company can execute documents brought together in one subsection rather than as currently drafted in two subsections. In Part 12, the Bill removes the requirement for a private company to have a secretary, which we discussed earlier. As a further step, it also, as the Minister mentioned, removes much of the official capacity of a secretary in any company that may choose to have a secretary but is not required to have one.

Section 36A(4) of the Companies Act 1985 allows any company to execute a document under the signature of,

    "a director and the secretary of a company, or by two directors of a company".

That position is retained in respect of public companies in this Bill by Clause 44(3)(a). However, there is no logical reason why the removal of the requirement for private companies to have a company secretary in this Bill—Clause 247—should prevent those companies that continue to have a secretary to execute documents under their signature. Indeed, it may be a hindrance for some companies not to have that capability. The amendment would retain that flexibility for those private companies that retain a secretary. If they do not have a secretary, they would have to execute documents under the signature of two directors or a director and a witness—Clause 44(3)(b) and (c).

The amendment seeks to give effect to this argument by allowing any company to validly execute a document by signature of a director and a secretary, if it has one. If a company does not have a secretary, obviously that procedure cannot be followed. However, seems no need for subsection (2) and, in the interests of brevity and deregulation, we seek its removal. That is the purpose of Amendments Nos. 43 and 44.

Amendment No. 53 concerns Clause 48, "Authentication of documents". This clause makes a change from the corresponding section—Section 41—of the Companies Act 1985, which included secretaries in the list of persons who may sufficiently authenticate documents by signature. Despite the Government's desire to minimise the number of companies who require secretaries, we see no reason why those companies who have secretaries should see their role diminished. The amendment therefore seeks to reinstate the meaning that we have lost from Section 41 of the Companies Act 1985. But, in the first instance, I beg to move Amendment No. 43.

Lord Razzall: I give general support to the point made by the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson. My noble friend Lord Sharman and I tabled Amendment No. 45, which has the same substance as the amendment tabled by the noble Lord. To a number of people, it seems strange that the legislation permits a public company which has a director, directors and a secretary to execute documents under the signature of
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column GC41
 
the director and the secretary; but where a private company has elected to retain the secretary, the provision will not be the same as for a public company, which would reflect the current position. Whatever wording the Minister thinks would be appropriate, that is the substance of this amendment.

6.15 pm

Lord McKenzie of Luton: We accept Amendments Nos. 44 and 49. Therefore, I will not need to refer to them any more.

Clause 44 is essentially deregulatory. It widens the options for a company executing a deed. As now, a company will be able to execute a deed either by affixing its seal, if it has one, or by the signature of two directors. Any company, both private and public, will also be able to do so by the signature of one director if witnessed by another person—for example, the secretary. So, whether or not the company has a secretary, the company will still be able to execute the deed, provided somebody witnesses a lone director's signature.

We need to consider the needs of both parties to the deed. The other party knows immediately from the name of the company whether it is a private or a public company, but it will not be immediately apparent whether it has a secretary. The distinction in this clause therefore is between public and private companies.

Any company that has a seal will continue to be able to set its own conditions for the affixing of its seal for the execution of documents. I understand that this is often delegated to the secretary alone for some categories of document. It will also continue to be open to the company to empower its secretary as its attorney to execute deeds on its behalf. There is no need for any special provision for private companies with company secretaries.

Compared with the present position, the clause introduces more flexibility for all companies, not less. It in no way hinders the execution of a document by a private company that chooses to continue to have a secretary. I therefore do not support Amendments Nos. 43, 46 or 47.

Amendment No. 53 is not needed as the clause enables any company which has a company secretary to authorise him or her to authenticate documents on behalf of the company.

The clause provides companies with greater flexibility than the present Act. The alternative to a director will be,

    "a person authorised by the company to act on its behalf".

Under the 1985 Act, this could be done only by a secretary or another authorised officer.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: I am grateful to the Minister for his response. The thrust of what he is saying seems to be that we will lose the secretarial role for private companies in the concept of an authorised officer. Lord McKenzie of Luton: The purpose of the amendment is wholly sound, but I will nevertheless resist it. Indeed, that protection should not apply only to the execution of documents. That is why Clause 257 provides:

    "In the case of a public company, a provision requiring or authorising a thing to be done by or to a director and the secretary of a company is not satisfied by its being done by or to the same person acting both as director and as, or in place of, the secretary".

The amendment is therefore wholly unnecessary as it is covered elsewhere.

Clause 45 [Common seal]:

On Question, whether Clause 45 shall stand part of the Bill?

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: I oppose the Motion that Clause 45 stand part of the Bill. The clause has the rather strange title, "Common seal", and even stranger wording:

    "A company may have a common seal, but need not have one".

It provides for companies to have a common seal, but I question the use of common seals for companies in the modern world. They appear to be no guarantee of authenticity; I am told that anyone can order a common seal of any design with no checks on their authority to do so. One could, theoretically, order the common seal of any company that one wished to and attempt to pass it off as an official common seal. It is now prevalent common practice for companies to execute documents by the methods in Clause 44(3), for example signing by two directors. The common seal is therefore superfluous in modern business.

We may be hanging on to an outdated tradition that no longer serves any useful purpose. If we are to be keen on deregulation, it would be straightforward to excise the clause from the Bill. The alternative would be to tighten up the laws relating to common seals, their acquisition and their use. I am sure that that is a step that the business community would not appreciate, and it is not one that we, in looking to create the company law of the future, should take.

Lord Clinton-Davis: Is it not enough to say that the company may have a common seal—full stop?

Lord McKenzie of Luton: The clause is needed to retain the existing option for companies of whether to have a seal. Following consultation on this issue in their 1998 report, The Execution of Deeds and Documents by or on behalf of Bodies Corporate, the Law Commission and the Scottish Law Commission recommended:

    "That the present 'dual system' should be retained, so that companies may continue to execute deeds either under the common seal or by the signature of relevant persons".

Without that clause, companies would no longer have a genuine choice whether to have a seal. In the consultation in 1996, the Law Commission sought views on whether the dual system for execution of deeds should be retained. In the consultation document, the Law Commission noted that while some consider execution under seal to be an archaic practice and an administrative burden, others value having execution under seal for the following reasons. It probably offers a greater guarantee of authenticity than execution by the signature of officers alone; it involves greater formality and may enhance the company's own controls; it may be required in some
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column GC44
 
jurisdictions; it may offer greater flexibility as non-directors may be authorised to affix the seal. The responses were almost unanimously in favour of retaining the dual system.

    "A company may have a common seal"—

    "but need not have one"?

Lord McKenzie of Luton: That just emphasises the point that there is an option; the company does not have to have one. As long as we are clear that that is the import of the Bill, the important thing is that that option is retained and available.

Lord McKenzie of Luton: I assure the Committee that the regulations under the clause do not provide power to require a company to change its name. An existing company would be affected by these regulations only if it were to seek to change its name so as to include a prescribed word or expressions.

The power in this clause is a restatement of the existing powers under Section 29(1)(a) of the 1985 Act. The existing regulations specify nearly 90 words and expressions that, when used in a company name, may imply business pre-eminence, a particular status or a specific function. They include chamber of commerce, charity, dental, duke, European, friendly society, Her Majesty, international, national, nurse, police, royal, stock exchange, trade union and Wales.

The aim is to ensure that the use of sensitive words is justified so that the public are not misled by the company's name. We intend to consult over the new regulations. These will update the existing regulations both by removing and adding words and expressions. No draft regulation is available at present. I assure the Committee that if a word is added to the list that is part of an existing company's name, that existing company would not be required to change its name: there is no power to require a change in such circumstances.

The noble Lord said: Amendment No. 62 seeks to align the clause with the corresponding provision in Section 19 of the Companies Act 1985.

As is referred to in paragraph 153 of the Explanatory Notes, this introduces a new requirement that a company limited by shares, which relies on a licence under Section 19 of the Companies Act 1985 in order to omit "limited" from its name, may have to change its constitution so as to prohibit the return of capital to its members if it wishes to continue to rely on the exemption. This has cost implications for such companies, although Clause 64(4) provides some relief in that it removes any requirement to obtain the consent of the Board of Trade or the Charity Commission for such a change. I note that a government amendment has been tabled which may have some impact on the measure, but this issue has been raised by the Law Society. I beg to move.

Lord McKenzie of Luton: This clause applies only to companies limited by shares that, by virtue of a licence, are exempt from the requirement for their name to conclude in "limited". There are just 21 GB companies of this type that are still active. This is partly because the provisions of the Companies Act 1948, under which licences were granted, were repealed in 1982. Those provisions applied to companies formed for promoting commerce, art, science, religion, charity or other public-benefit type objects.

So this clause is about companies limited by shares that are not-for-profit companies. It is inappropriate for them to pay dividends to their members. For similar reasons it is inappropriate for them to buy back their members' shares or return capital to them in other ways.

The issue of returning capital does not arise under the current law. This is because, if a company wants to reduce its capital or buy back its own shares, it needs special provision in its articles. However, companies with a licence under the 1948 Act cannot usually amend their articles without permission from the DTI.

Two bits of deregulation in the Bill affect this. The first is that companies will be able to reduce their capital and buy back their own shares without special provision in their articles. The second is in Clause 64. We are doing away with the need for exempt companies to apply to the DTI for approval when they amend their articles.

There is a steady stream of applications to the DTI from exempt companies seeking to change their articles. Clause 64 represents useful deregulation for those companies. However, to ensure that exempt

companies do not act in a way that is inconsistent with their status, we have added the words that the amendment seeks to remove.

By contrast, Clause 77 provides that a company may change its name not only by special resolution but also by other means provided for in the company's articles. Clause 65 expressly provides the directors with the power to change the company's name in these circumstances to ensure that it is possible for the company to comply with the direction in the specified period. But, just because the company has been directed to change its name, it should not be deprived of the freedom to change its name by other means.

30 Jan 2006 : Column GC55
 
other than perhaps at a symbolic level. Therefore, we seek its removal on both practical and deregulatory grounds.

First, the powers in the clause are very wide. Secondly, the powers are in any case ineffective; even if a name is not available, there is nothing to stop a company, registered under one name, from trading under another. Therefore, the Secretary of State can direct a company to change its registered name under the power contained in Clause 68, but that might have no effect on the name that the company chooses to trade under and it is, of course, the trading name, not the registered name, where the commercial issues and interests lie. Thirdly, why does the Secretary of State need this power? If company "A" believes that its operations and profitability have been damaged by company "B" seeking to pass itself off as company "A", it can bring a civil case for damages.

The information we received during our consultation on that section is that it is, in practice, hard to persuade the registrar to take any action whatever in that regard. So, on the grounds of deregulation, of effectiveness, of the availability of alternative legal remedies and of use, we seek the removal of the clause.

Lord McKenzie of Luton: I shall deal with both issues together. Regarding the statistics, less than one per cent of all name changes are as a result of the direction. Between April and November last year, 166 companies were directed to change their names because they were too much like another's.

The clause, like its predecessor in the 1985 Act, is there to prevent confusion between very similar names on the registrar's index. We propose to make regulations under Clause 67 to deal with some common, minor differences between names. For example, under the current law, "Smith Limited" and "Smith and Co. Limited" are taken as the same and only one can be registered. However, it is impossible to provide for all minor differences in this mechanical way. That is why we need Clause 68. It operates after a company name has been registered and allows the Secretary of State to direct changes of name.

The clause provides a power for the Secretary of State to make rules about proceedings before a company names adjudicator.     "The rules may . . . make provision".

I return to the familiar issue of why "may" and not "shall". It is hard to see that the adjudicator could work without the powers granted in paragraphs (a) to (k). If one or more were missed out, he could be hamstrung. To make sure that he can be fully effective, we seek to replace "may" with "shall".

As I have said, this is a familiar case, but I make no apology for returning to it. Ministers may argue that "may" and "shall" are interchangeable. They pray in aid parliamentary draftsmen. I do not accept that argument; if I say I may do something it is not the same as when I say I shall do something. If parliamentary draftsmen argue that it is the same, they and I live in different worlds; which is probably an accurate reflection of the truth.

to (k). I do not accept that; the inclusion of the phrase "in particular" means that what follows is not an exclusive list. I beg to move.

Written Statements

Army: Future Structures

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Ministry of Defence (Lord Drayson): My right honourable friend the Secretary of State for Defence (John Reid) has made the following Written Ministerial Statement.

My predecessor announced to the House in December 2004 (Official Report, col. 1795), that under the Future Army Structures, we intend to re-role an Armoured Brigade to a Mechanised Brigade and a Mechanised Brigade to a Light Brigade.

I am now in a position to confirm that 4th Armoured Brigade will re-role to a Mechanised Brigade in 2006 and will move from its current base in Osnabruck garrison in Germany to Catterick in 2008–09. A number of other minor adjustments will also be made. I have notified the German Government of our plans. 19 Brigade will re-role to a Light Brigade and will subsequently relocate away from Catterick to Scotland and Northern Ireland, although decisions on the final location of individual units have yet to be taken. We hope to be in a position to announce this later in the year. The net effect will be a reduction of our forces in Germany by roughly 2,200 personnel.

While there may be further modest adjustments to our force levels in Germany in the coming years where this makes sense, I would envisage sizeable forces remaining there for the long-term, where they will continue to enjoy excellent relations with the local population and contribute to our strong bilateral relationship with the German Government.

EU: Employment and Social Policy, Health and Consumer Affairs Council

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Department for Work and Pensions (Lord Hunt of Kings Heath): My honourable friend the Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State for Work and Pensions (James Plaskitt) has made the following Statement.

The Employment and Social Policy, Health and Consumer Affairs Informal Council was held on 19–21 January in Villach, Austria.

My honourable friend the Minister for Employment Relations and Consumer Affairs (Gerry Sutcliffe) and I attended the working session for the UK.

The Austrian presidency posed two topics for discussion on Friday 20 January. The morning session discussed "Flexicurity—flexibility through Security". The afternoon session discussed "The Social dimension of the revised Lisbon strategy and the streamlining of social policy processes".

My honourable friend represented the UK during the morning discussion of "Flexicurity". This session explored new ways of balancing flexibility and social and employment security in Europe.

Member States took examples from their national reform programmes to illustrate the balance they had struck between flexibility and security. For the UK, my honourable friend suggested that legislative measures were for the national level and the EU could add value by sharing experience and good practice and by promoting better regulation principles.

The Commissioner noted that "flexicurity" would be addressed in the Commission green paper on labour law, due in the first half of the year.

Overall, Ministers agreed that there is no one size fits all model for flexicurity, but disagreed whether legislative action should be taken at the EU level.

The afternoon session asked Ministers to focus on the presidency question about the social dimension of the strategy for growth and jobs and the desired role and outputs for streamlined open method of co-ordination of social policies.

For the UK, I called for practical discussion about real policies rather than theoretical debate of principles. I suggested that the aim should be to integrate social policy making within an overall reform strategy where it could contribute to the delivery of employment aims.

There was a consensus that a visible social dimension to the Lisbon strategy must be maintained and that streamlining open method of co-ordination would help.

The presidency then proposed that Employment Ministers should feed these messages into the spring European Council.
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column WS5
 

Far East PoW and Civilian Internees Scheme

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Ministry of Defence (Lord Drayson): My honourable friend the Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State for Defence (Don Touhig) has made the following Written Ministerial Statement.

In my Statement on 12 December, following my appearance at the Public Administration Select Committee oral evidence session on the operation of the FEPOW Scheme, I told the House that I had commissioned a review into the extent to which inconsistent eligibility criteria had been used for deciding claims before and after the introduction of the birth-link criterion; this work is well-advanced. In addition, I said that a separate, independent investigation would be conducted into how the use of inconsistent criteria had arisen and why it had not been exposed earlier. I said that this work would be undertaken by a retired senior official from outside of the departments involved. I am pleased to announce that Mr David Watkins, a retired senior civil servant formerly in the Northern Ireland Office who is well qualified for the task, has agreed to undertake the investigation. His role will be to consider:

    how the original inconsistencies arose, identifying any specific shortcomings in the development and implementation of policy;

    how, subsequently, the departments involved failed to identify that there had been inconsistencies, despite the need to explain the Government's position in Parliament, in the courts and to the Parliamentary Ombudsman; and

    whether there are any lessons to be learnt for the future from such shortcomings as are identified in the development of the scheme and in its administration.

Mr Watkins has begun his investigation and I have indicated my concern that, while it must be thorough, it should be completed as quickly as practicable.

I will make a further statement to Parliament when I have received Mr Watkins' final report.

Olympic Games 2012: Olympic Park Master Plan

Lord Davies of Oldham: My right honourable friend the Secretary of State for Culture, Media and Sport (Tessa Jowell) has made the following Written Ministerial Statement.

The master plan for the Olympic Park was largely developed in the second half of 2003 by the EDAW consortium and outline planning permissions were secured in autumn 2004. The candidate file submitted to the International Olympic Committee (IOC) in November 2004 and subsequently presented to the Evaluation Commission in February 2005 presented the concept of a compact Games with the Olympic Park at its heart.
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column WS6
 

Since winning the right to stage the Olympic Games and Paralympic Games on 6 July 2005, the master plan has been reviewed, as part of the normal development process for a major project. This review has resulted in changes that will deliver a more compact Olympic Park, while meeting the technical specifications of the IOC. In summary, the revised master plan will:

    free up some land that previously had been required for the Olympic Park;

    avoid the relocation of about 100 businesses, more than 70 homes and a place of worship, and safeguard more than 1200 jobs;

    reduce overall programme and delivery risk;

    enhance operational security, needed after the terrorist attacks on 7 July 2005; and

    be delivered with a potential cost reduction over current estimates of between £250 million and £350 million.

A detailed review has been undertaken of the Olympic village and the core Olympic and Paralympic requirements to improve deliverability, efficiency of design, utilisation, reduce risk and costs and smooth the way for best legacy use.

The revised plan therefore proposes realigning the village to locate approximately 80 per cent of it on the Stratford Rail lands immediately to the north of the Channel Tunnel Rail Link (CTRL).

The proposed building envelope and mix of uses complies with Stratford City's existing planning permissions and will provide a village of a similar high standard to that proposed in the original master plan.

This move will enable an earlier start on the village, allowing building work on almost half of it to begin before power lines have been placed underground.

The revised plan is intended to offer improved security through:

    the relocation of the IBC and MPC within the Olympic Park security cordon, bringing those facilities closer to the centre of the Olympic Park and away from major roads and unsecured car parks;

    integrated security measures for Stratford City and the Olympic Games should enable the concurrent construction of the projects to progress more efficiently; and

    a more compact athletes village should improve the efficiency of the security operation.

Further work on refining and developing the security aspects of the revised plan is in hand.

Details of the revisions to the master plan are set out in maps to be placed in the Library of the House.
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column WS7

Afghanistan: DfID Expenditure

Lord Elton asked Her Majesty's Government:

    How much has been expended by the Department for International Development in pursuit of their policies in Afghanistan (a) in the last period of 12 months for which figures are available; and (b) since the commencement of their reconstruction programme in Afghanistan. [HL3521]

The Lord President of the Council (Baroness Amos): In 2003–05 the Department for International Development (DfID) spent a total of £79.6 million in Afghanistan bilaterally. The bilateral programme budget for Afghanistan in 2005–06 is £100 million. This has been fully committed and over £75 million has already been disbursed. By the end of 2005–06 DfID will have spent approximately £390 million in Afghanistan bilaterally, since beginning its reconstruction programme in 2000–01 (this includes humanitarian spending).

In addition to its bilateral programmes in Afghanistan, DfID provides 19 per cent. of the European Commission's commitment of €1 billion (2002–07), and 10.4 per cent. of the World Bank's commitment, currently some $270 million a year. DfID also contributes globally to UN programmes active in Afghanistan, including those of the United Nations Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR), the United Nations Development Programme (UNDP), the World Food Programme, and through the Asian Development Bank.

Extraordinary Rendition

Lord Tebbit asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Whether they refuse to receive or use intelligence gained from Central Intelligence Agency prisoners subject to rendition where it relates to the prevention of terrorism in the United Kingdom. [HL2775]

The Minister of State, Home Office (Baroness Scotland of Asthal): The intelligence and security agencies receive large volumes of material from disparate sources, all of which is evaluated with a view to forming judgments designed to protect national security. They consider, for instance, where the information comes from, how it was obtained, the motivation of the source, and what kind of reporting the source has. Where a report is known to derive from a source under detention, that too would be taken into account. As with all intelligence information, each individual piece is considered against the backdrop of a body of other material and used to inform an overall evaluation.

Food Standards Agency: Vitamin D

Earl Howe asked Her Majesty's Government:

    What response the Food Standards Agency has taken of the recent removal of vitamin D from a
     

30 Jan 2006 : Column WA7
 
    number of popular breakfast cereals by Kellogg's; and what representations have been made to that manufacturer on the issue; and [HL3548]

    In the light of recent research findings on the benefits of vitamin D in reducing the risk of a number of cancers and chronic medical conditions, what plans they and the Food Standards Agency have to promulgate appropriate public health messages on the issue, most particularly among Afro-Caribbean communities. [HL3549]

The Minister of State, Department of Health (Lord Warner): As far as the Government are aware Kellogg's has not changed the fortification profiles, with respect to vitamin D, on any of its major breakfast cereal products since 1998. We understand that vitamin D was removed from Cornflakes in 1998, in order to standardise the product across Europe.

The Committee on Medical Aspects of Nutrition Policy advised in 1998 on the health benefits of vitamin D in relation to bone health. The relationship between vitamin D and cancer and other chronic medical conditions is less clear.

Most of the population are able to make sufficient vitamin D through the action of sunlight on the skin. The Food Standards Agency advises pregnant and breastfeeding women, older people, those of Asian origin, those who always cover up their skin when outside, rarely get outdoors or eat no meat or oily fish to take vitamin D supplements. Some ethnic minority groups, especially if they have pigmented skin and choose diets which exclude meat and fish, may also be vulnerable to low vitamin D status. This may include some individuals of Afro-Caribbean descent.

    Whether accommodation consisting of a pitch on a caravan site, provided by a non-metropolitan council in England within the meaning of the Local Government Act 1972, is exempt from rent regulation by virtue of paragraphs 10(1)(b) and (6) ("exempt accommodation" head (ii)) of the Housing Benefit (General) Amendment Regulations 1995 (S.I. 1644/1995), as amended by paragraph 17 of the Social Security (Miscellaneous Amendments) Regulations 2003 (S.I. 363/2003); and, if so, whether they will circularise county councils to ensure that Gypsies and Travellers who qualify under these provisions are not subject to rent regulation. Pensions: Direct Payment

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Department for Work and Pensions (Lord Hunt of Kings Heath): As at 23 December 2005, 7,459,895 state pension accounts were being paid by direct payment into a bank/building society and 1,886,499 accounts were being paid by direct payment into a Post Office card account.

Payment into an account became the department's normal method of payment from April 2003. However, the department has been making payments into customers' bank/building society accounts since the early 1980s. The Post Office card account became available for use from 1 April 2003.
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column WA13
 

In line with our wider policy on financial inclusion, we have always made it clear that payment into a bank or building society account is the best option for the overwhelming majority of customers, rather than payment into a Post Office card account. The Post Office card account is a simple account with limited functions. It can only receive payments of benefits, pensions and tax credits. It does not, for example, allow customers to make savings on fuel bills by paying by direct debit, it cannot receive payments of private pensions, cheques cannot be paid in to the Post Office card account and it does not pay interest on balances.

Baroness Byford asked Her Majesty's Government:

    Whether the Department for Work and Pensions gives its employees who deal with state pensions guidance to encourage applicants to choose to receive their payments via their bank account. [HL3539]

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Department for Work and Pensions (Lord Hunt of Kings Heath): Direct payment is the department's normal method of payment.

The department provides guidance to staff that deal with state retirement pension to enable them to give all applicants for state retirement pension full information on the payment options available.

In line with our wider policy on financial inclusion, we have always made it clear that payment into a bank or building society account is the best option for the overwhelming majority of customers, rather than payment into a Post Office card account. The Post Office card account is a simple account with limited functions. It can only receive payments of benefits, pensions and tax credits. It does not, for example, allow customers to make savings on fuel bills by paying by direct debit, it cannot receive payments of private pensions, cheques cannot be paid in to the Post Office card account and it does not pay interest on balances. The role of staff is to provide factual information to enable the applicant to choose the account that best suits their needs.

Squirrels

Earl Peel asked Her Majesty's Government:

    In which places in the United Kingdom grey squirrel controls have been deemed successful. [HL3524]

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Department for Environment, Food and Rural Affairs (Lord Bach): We have limited information on the success of squirrel control on private land as there is no requirement on landowners to report their squirrel control activities. Where control is undertaken using good practice it is most likely to succeed, but this is not always the case.

In England, the Forestry Commission recently reviewed its grant-supported grey squirrel control and concluded that the level of support available to individual owners was insufficient to ensure that control met good practice standards and brought in question success in terms of damage control.

The recently published Grey Squirrels and England's Woodlands policy and action statement aims to address this issue by targeting existing resources in areas where they are most needed and at a level that will help to enable good practice to be followed.

World Health Organisation

Lord Steel of Aikwood asked Her Majesty's Government:

    When the governing body of the World Health Organisation (WHO) is next due to meet; who will be their representative at the meeting; and whether they will support the admission of Taiwan as an observer member of the WHO. [HL3322]

The Minister of State, Department of Health (Lord Warner): The Executive Board of the World Health Organisation is due to meet from 23–28 January 2006. At this meeting members will agree on the agenda for the forthcoming World Health Assembly (WHA), which is to be held 22–27 May 2006. The United Kingdom delegations for both meetings are still to be finalised but the UK is traditionally led by a Health Minister and/or the Chief Medical Officer.

The Government have a clear understanding of Taiwan's desire for membership of the WHO and
 
30 Jan 2006 : Column WA20
 
integration into the world healthcare community. However, there are difficulties over Taiwanese involvement in a United Nation forum where statehood is a prerequisite for full membership.

The UK, along with other European Union member states, strongly supports the principle enshrined in the WHO constitution that the enjoyment of the highest attainable standard of health is one of the fundamental rights of every human being. The EU has, on a prior occasion, expressed its hope that "the Secretariat, and others organising meetings and working groups under WHO auspices, will show flexibility in finding mechanisms to allow Taiwanese medical and public health officials to participate in these activities".

However, observer status, of and by itself, does not automatically confer any rights or benefits on the holder; these have to be negotiated separately. The EU has therefore chosen instead to press for practical measures to allow Taiwan to participate in the activities of the WHO.

Immediately prior to the 2005 WHA meeting, it was announced that the WHO Secretariat and China had agreed a confidential Memorandum of Understanding covering Taiwan's participation in the activities of the WHO. There are signs that it is having a practical effect. For example, health officials from Taiwan attended a WHO conference on avian flu in Geneva in mid-November and representatives from Taiwan attended an Asia Pacific Economic Co-operation avian flu conference in Australia from 31 October– 1 November.

We shall continue to watch developments closely, in particular to make sure that the MoU continues to enable Taiwan's participation in relevant activities. With EU partners, we will push for further action if necessary.
